* **2004 — MapReduce: Simplified Data Processing on Large Clusters** (paper)— *Jeffrey Dean* and *Sanjay Ghemawat* present a programming model and runtime for large-scale data processing; best for the historical foundation of modern big-data systems. [Link](https://static.googleusercontent.com/media/research.google.com/en//archive/mapreduce-osdi04.pdf)

* **2006 — Bigtable: A Distributed Storage System for Structured Data** (paper)— *Fay Chang* and collaborators describe Google’s distributed storage system for structured data; best for the design lineage of wide-column distributed databases. [Link](https://static.googleusercontent.com/media/research.google.com/en//archive/bigtable-osdi06.pdf)

* **2006 — A Fast Learning Algorithm for Deep Belief Nets** (paper)— *Geoffrey E. Hinton*, *Simon Osindero*, and *Yee-Whye Teh* revive deep neural-network training through deep belief nets; best for the bridge into modern deep learning. [Link](https://doi.org/10.1162/neco.2006.18.7.1527)

* **2007 — Dynamo: Amazon’s Highly Available Key-value Store** (paper)— *Giuseppe DeCandia* and collaborators describe Amazon’s eventually consistent key-value store; best for production distributed storage tradeoffs. [Link](https://www.allthingsdistributed.com/files/amazon-dynamo-sosp2007.pdf)

* **2008 — Bitcoin: A Peer-to-Peer Electronic Cash System** (white paper)— *Satoshi Nakamoto* combines proof of work, peer-to-peer networking, and a public ledger into Bitcoin; best for the origin of blockchain-based cryptocurrency systems. [Link](https://bitcoin.org/bitcoin.pdf)

* **2010 — Dremel: Interactive Analysis of Web-Scale Datasets** (paper)— *Sergey Melnik* and collaborators describe Google’s columnar interactive analytics system; best for the lineage of modern large-scale analytical query systems. [Link](https://research.google/pubs/dremel-interactive-analysis-of-web-scale-datasets-2/)

* **2012 — ImageNet Classification with Deep Convolutional Neural Networks** (paper)— *Alex Krizhevsky*, *Ilya Sutskever*, and *Geoffrey E. Hinton* demonstrate a major deep-learning breakthrough on ImageNet; best for the modern deep-learning turning point in computer vision. [Link](https://proceedings.neurips.cc/paper/2012/hash/c399862d3b9d6b76c8436e924a68c45b-Abstract.html)

* **2013 — Efficient Estimation of Word Representations in Vector Space** (paper)— *Tomas Mikolov*, *Kai Chen*, *Greg Corrado*, and *Jeffrey Dean* introduce efficient word-vector learning methods; best for the neural embedding wave in NLP. [Link](https://arxiv.org/abs/1301.3781)

* **2014 — In Search of an Understandable Consensus Algorithm** (paper)— *Diego Ongaro* and *John Ousterhout* present Raft as a more understandable consensus algorithm; best for learning modern replicated-log consensus design. [Link](https://raft.github.io/raft.pdf)

* **2014 — Generative Adversarial Nets** (paper)— *Ian Goodfellow* and collaborators introduce adversarial training for generative modeling; best for the origin of GANs. [Link](https://arxiv.org/abs/1406.2661)

* **2015 — Deep Residual Learning for Image Recognition** (paper)— *Kaiming He*, *Xiangyu Zhang*, *Shaoqing Ren*, and *Jian Sun* introduce residual networks; best for the architecture breakthrough that enabled much deeper neural networks. [Link](https://arxiv.org/abs/1512.03385)

* **2016 — Mastering the Game of Go with Deep Neural Networks and Tree Search** (paper)— *David Silver* and collaborators describe AlphaGo’s combination of deep learning and search; best for a landmark AI system in strategic decision-making. [Link](https://doi.org/10.1038/nature16961)

* **2017 — Attention Is All You Need** (paper)— *Ashish Vaswani* and collaborators introduce the Transformer architecture; best for the architectural origin of modern large language models and many foundation models. [Link](https://arxiv.org/abs/1706.03762)

* **2018 — BERT: Pre-training of Deep Bidirectional Transformers for Language Understanding** (paper)— *Jacob Devlin*, *Ming-Wei Chang*, *Kenton Lee*, and *Kristina Toutanova* introduce bidirectional Transformer pretraining for NLP; best for the pre-LLM transformer language-understanding phase. [Link](https://arxiv.org/abs/1810.04805)

* **2020 — Language Models are Few-Shot Learners** (paper)— *Tom B. Brown* and collaborators present GPT-3 and few-shot prompting behavior at scale; best for the emergence of scaling-centered language-model research. [Link](https://arxiv.org/abs/2005.14165)

* **2021 — Highly Accurate Protein Structure Prediction with AlphaFold** (paper)— *John Jumper* and collaborators describe AlphaFold2’s protein-structure prediction system; best for a landmark example of deep learning transforming a scientific domain. [Link](https://doi.org/10.1038/s41586-021-03819-2)

* **2022 — Training Language Models to Follow Instructions with Human Feedback** (paper)— *Long Ouyang* and collaborators describe instruction tuning and reinforcement learning from human feedback for language models; best for understanding the instruction-following turn in LLM systems. [Link](https://arxiv.org/abs/2203.02155)

* **2022 — High-Resolution Image Synthesis with Latent Diffusion Models** (paper)— *Robin Rombach*, *Andreas Blattmann*, *Dominik Lorenz*, *Patrick Esser*, and *Björn Ommer* present latent diffusion models for efficient image generation; best for the diffusion-model lineage behind modern text-to-image systems. [Link](https://arxiv.org/abs/2112.10752)

{% comment %}

## Specifications for the Computer Science Resource Atlas

### 1. Core Identity

This resource is a **Computer Science Resource Atlas**, not a curriculum, roadmap, textbook guide, or career guide.

Its purpose is to collect **high-value CS resources** and organize them into a navigable resource ocean. It should help readers discover excellent books, courses, papers, project collections, documentation, standards, research gateways, and technical references, while preserving freedom of navigation.

It should not tell readers “you must learn A before B.” It should instead show **what exists, what each resource is good for, and where it belongs**.

---

### 2. Organizing Principle

The atlas uses a two-level structure:

```md
## Technical Category

### Functional Subcategory

- Resource entry
- Resource entry
- Resource entry
```

**Technical Category** is the broad CS area, such as algorithms, systems, databases, AI, security, or HCI.

**Functional Subcategory** is determined by the actual role of the resources inside that chapter. It is not a fixed template. For example:

In **General CS**, useful subcategories include **Curriculum Maps**, **Self-Study Curricula**, **Broad Introductory Courses**, **Whole-Stack Understanding**, **Research Practice**, and **Historical Foundations**.

In **Algorithms**, useful subcategories include **Core Courses**, **Textbooks**, **Data Structures**, **Complexity Theory**, **Competitive Programming**, **Advanced Algorithms**, **Specialized Areas**, and **Research Gateways**.

In **Systems**, useful subcategories include **Architecture**, **Operating Systems**, **Toolchains**, **Debugging**, **Performance**, **Concurrency**, **Runtime Systems**, and **Systems Labs**.

This structure is consistent with how official CS mappings separate the field into knowledge areas and taxonomies, while still allowing a reader-facing classification that is more usable than a formal ontology. **CS2023** is useful as a reference for broad CS knowledge areas, while **ACM CCS** is useful as a formal research-area taxonomy. ([CSED][1])

---

### 3. Entry Format

Every resource entry uses one line.

```md
- ★ **Resource Name** (resource type)— *Author / Organization* one-sentence description; best for X. [Link]
```

Required elements:

| Element                   | Rule                                                                                                       |
| ------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| **Marker**                | Optional: ★, ☆, ✨, or none                                                                                 |
| **Resource Name**         | Bold                                                                                                       |
| **Resource Type**         | Parentheses immediately after the name                                                                     |
| **Author / Organization** | Italic                                                                                                     |
| **Description**           | One sentence only                                                                                          |
| **Best for**              | Must say what kind of reader or use case fits                                                              |
| **Link**                  | Use official, author, university, publisher, GitHub, arXiv, standards, or project source whenever possible |

Example:

```md
- ★ **Programming Languages: Application and Interpretation** (textbook)— *Shriram Krishnamurthi* uses interpreters to explain language features; best for learning PL concepts by building them. [Link]
```

---

### 4. Resource Type Vocabulary

Use a controlled but flexible vocabulary.

| Type                   | Meaning                                                            |
| ---------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------ |
| **textbook**           | Structured book for systematic learning                            |
| **course**             | Lecture-based or assignment-based educational sequence             |
| **book**               | General book, not necessarily textbook-like                        |
| **paper**              | Individual research or foundational paper                          |
| **paper collection**   | Curated set of papers                                              |
| **reading list**       | Course or researcher-curated reading list                          |
| **documentation**      | Official docs for a language, tool, framework, platform, or system |
| **specification**      | Formal standard, protocol, language spec, API spec                 |
| **reference**          | Lookup-oriented resource                                           |
| **tutorial**           | Guided learning material for a specific topic or tool              |
| **guide**              | Practical instruction or conceptual guide                          |
| **handbook**           | Broad practical reference                                          |
| **project collection** | Many implementation projects or build-your-own materials           |
| **lab sequence**       | Assignment-heavy practical learning sequence                       |
| **visualization tool** | Interactive or visual learning tool                                |
| **research archive**   | Historical or research-oriented resource archive                   |
| **conference**         | Research venue                                                     |
| **journal**            | Research publication venue                                         |
| **portal**             | Gateway to a field, project, standard, or ecosystem                |
| **taxonomy**           | Classification system                                              |
| **curriculum report**  | Formal curriculum or education standard                            |
| **self-study guide**   | Curated study plan or independent learning guide                   |

Do not over-invent types. If a resource fits multiple types, choose the most useful type for the reader.

---

### 5. Marker System

Markers are used sparingly.

| Marker    | Meaning                                                          | Rule                        |
| --------- | ---------------------------------------------------------------- | --------------------------- |
| ★         | Best learning resource in this functional subcategory            | Usually 1–3 per subcategory |
| ☆         | Strong alternative, companion, or advanced second choice         | Usually 1–3 per subcategory |
| ✨         | Promising, interesting, or high-potential but not fully verified | Use rarely                  |
| No marker | High-value resource, but not singled out                         | Default state               |

The marker does not mean “good” versus “bad.” All listed resources should be good enough to include. The marker only indicates **relative learning value inside that subcategory**.

**★** should be given to resources that are clear, durable, pedagogically strong, and useful to many readers.

**☆** should be given to resources that are excellent but more specialized, more advanced, more supplementary, or less universal.

**✨** should be used for resources that appear valuable but are less verified, newer, niche, unstable, or exploratory.

---

### 6. Quality Standard

A resource should be included only if it satisfies at least one of these conditions:

| Inclusion Reason           | Examples                                                                        |
| -------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| **Canonical**              | Widely recognized textbook, paper, course, or standard                          |
| **Official**               | Maintained by the language, tool, framework, or standards body                  |
| **Authoritative**          | Produced by a leading researcher, university, lab, or professional organization |
| **Pedagogically strong**   | Excellent for learning, even if not historically canonical                      |
| **Practically valuable**   | Helps build, debug, test, deploy, or inspect real systems                       |
| **Research-useful**        | Opens a field, literature, venue, or research tradition                         |
| **Historically important** | Shows conceptual origin or major turning point                                  |
| **Open and accessible**    | Free, author-open, official-open, or public educational material                |

No quantity limit exists. The only filter is **quality**.

---

### 7. Link Policy

Use the cleanest legitimate link.

Priority order:

1. **Official project / author page**
2. **University course page**
3. **Official documentation**
4. **Publisher page**
5. **Author-open PDF**
6. **arXiv / institutional repository**
7. **GitHub repository**
8. **Standards body / RFC / specification page**
9. **Stable archive only when legitimate and necessary**

Avoid unofficial PDF mirrors when an official page exists. For books that are not legally free, link to the publisher, author page, or official book page. For open resources, link directly to the official open version.

---

### 8. Description Style

Each entry must be **one sentence**.

The sentence should answer:

**What is it?**
**Who made it?**
**What is it best for?**

Preferred pattern:

```md
- **Name** (type)— *Author* does X; best for Y. [Link]
```

Avoid:

Long summaries.
Multiple sentences.
Marketing language.
Personal praise.
“Must-read” unless truly canonical.
Unclear claims like “great resource for everyone.”
Over-specific reading instructions.

Good:

```md
- ★ **Operating Systems: Three Easy Pieces** (textbook)— *Remzi H. Arpaci-Dusseau* and *Andrea C. Arpaci-Dusseau* organize OS concepts around virtualization, concurrency, and persistence; best for a clear first operating-systems textbook. [Link]
```

Bad:

```md
- ★ OSTEP — This is one of the best OS books ever written and everyone should read it carefully from beginning to end because it explains many things very well.
```

---

### 9. Chapter Design Rules

Each chapter begins with a short paragraph explaining scope.

```md
## 6. Networking, Distributed Systems, and Cloud

This chapter collects resources on computer networking, Internet protocols, network programming, distributed systems, consensus, cloud infrastructure, containers, Kubernetes, service reliability, and cloud-native operations.
```

Then use content-specific functional subcategories.

Do not force every chapter into the same subcategory template. The internal structure should reflect the field.

Each subcategory should group resources by **use function**, not by prestige.

Examples:

Good:

```md
### 6.4 Distributed Systems Foundations
### 6.5 Consensus, Replication, and Consistency
### 6.6 Distributed Systems Testing, Failure Analysis, and Case Studies
```

Less good:

```md
### Books
### Courses
### Papers
### Websites
```

Resource type is already visible inside each entry, so subcategories should not merely repeat type.

---

### 10. Duplicate and Placement Rules

A resource should appear as a full entry **only once** in the atlas.

If a resource is relevant to multiple chapters, place it in the chapter where its **primary use** belongs. Other chapters may mention it only in **Duplicate / Placement Notes**.

Placement rule:

| Resource Kind                     | Put It Where                                                 |
| --------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------ |
| General self-study map            | General CS                                                   |
| Language official docs            | Programming / language-specific chapter                      |
| Build-your-own collection         | Practice Projects chapter                                    |
| Whole-stack resource              | General CS or Systems, depending on emphasis                 |
| Systems performance resource      | Systems                                                      |
| Distributed data systems resource | Databases or Distributed Systems, based on center            |
| Messaging/integration resource    | Networking / Distributed Systems                             |
| Research venue                    | Most relevant technical chapter                              |
| Paper archive spanning all CS     | Paper Collections / Research Discovery                       |
| Documentation methodology         | Software Construction or Research Practice, depending on use |

At the end of a chapter, include a **Duplicate / Placement Notes** section when needed.

Example:

```md
### Duplicate / Placement Notes

- **Do not duplicate _Designing Data-Intensive Applications_ here.** It belongs in Chapter 7 because its center is data systems, storage, replication, indexing, streams, and transactions.
- **Move _Build Your Own X_ to Chapter 14.** It should appear as a full entry only in the practice-project chapter, with cross-reference mentions elsewhere.
```

---

### 11. Star Assignment Rules

Stars are assigned locally within a functional subcategory.

A resource gets ★ if it is probably the best first serious learning choice for that subcategory.

A resource gets ☆ if it is excellent but less universal, more advanced, more specialized, or better as a companion.

Do not mark every famous resource.

For example, in **Algorithms / Core Textbooks**:

```md
- ★ **Algorithms** (textbook)— *Jeff Erickson* provides a free, rigorous, modern algorithms textbook; best for learners who want depth, proofs, and high-quality exposition. [Link]
- ★ **Introduction to Algorithms** (textbook)— *Thomas H. Cormen*, *Charles E. Leiserson*, *Ronald L. Rivest*, and *Clifford Stein* provide the standard broad reference; best as a comprehensive reference rather than a light first read. [Link]
- ☆ **Algorithm Design** (textbook)— *Jon Kleinberg* and *Éva Tardos* emphasize design techniques and proof; best for building design judgment through well-chosen problems. [Link]
```

Even if more resources are excellent, most should remain unmarked.

---

### 12. Source Mixture Rules

Each technical chapter should ideally include a healthy mixture of:

| Resource Mode          | Purpose                                    |
| ---------------------- | ------------------------------------------ |
| **Courses**            | Structured learning                        |
| **Textbooks**          | Deep conceptual development                |
| **Documentation**      | Living technical reference                 |
| **Specifications**     | Precise standards and protocols            |
| **Projects / labs**    | Practice and implementation                |
| **Papers**             | Foundational or research ideas             |
| **Research venues**    | Entry into current research                |
| **Tools**              | Practical inspection, testing, or building |
| **Archives / indexes** | Discovery and historical depth             |

This follows the same documentation insight as **Diátaxis**: different resource forms answer different user needs. Diátaxis separates documentation into tutorials, how-to guides, reference, and explanation; this atlas similarly separates resources by function instead of flattening them into one undifferentiated list. ([Diátaxis][2])

---

### 13. Scope Boundaries

The atlas should include:

CS foundations.
Programming and software construction.
Programming languages and formal methods.
Algorithms and theory.
Systems and infrastructure.
Networks, distributed systems, and cloud.
Databases, storage, and information retrieval.
Security, privacy, and cryptography.
Mathematics for CS.
AI, ML, data science, and foundation models.
NLP, vision, robotics, graphics, and specialized AI.
HCI, visualization, and socio-technical computing.
Web development and application engineering.
Practice projects.
Paper collections and research discovery.
Documentation, standards, and living references.
Classic archives and historical resources.

It should not become:

A ranked curriculum.
A career roadmap.
A shopping list.
A list of every possible course.
A link dump with no structure.
A pirated-PDF index.
A “best books only” list.
A personal reading diary.

---

### 14. Chapter Ending: Duplicate / Placement Notes

Every chapter may end with this section when relevant:

```md
### Duplicate / Placement Notes

- ...
- ...
```

Use it to record decisions such as:

Move this resource to a later chapter.
Do not duplicate this resource here.
Keep only as cross-reference.
This resource belongs better in a general chapter.
This resource should be archived or removed.

This prevents silent duplication and keeps the atlas maintainable.

---

### 15. Final Formatting Rules

Use this exact typography:

| Element               | Formatting                        |
| --------------------- | --------------------------------- |
| Resource title        | `**bold**`                        |
| Resource type         | `(type)` after title              |
| Author / organization | `*italic*`                        |
| Marker                | Before title                      |
| Link                  | `[Link]` at end                   |
| Subcategory           | `###`                             |
| Chapter               | `##`                              |
| Duplicate notes       | `### Duplicate / Placement Notes` |

Final entry pattern:

```md
- ★ **Resource Name** (resource type)— *Author / Organization* one-sentence description; best for X. [Link]
```

---

### 16. Quality Checklist Before Adding a Resource

Before adding a resource, check:

```md
- Does it belong in this chapter rather than another one?
- Is it high-value enough to survive long-term?
- Is the link official, author-open, university-based, publisher-based, standards-based, or otherwise stable?
- Is the description only one sentence?
- Does the sentence say what the resource is best for?
- Is the star marker necessary, or should it remain unmarked?
- Is this resource already listed elsewhere?
- If duplicated, should it be moved, deleted, or mentioned only in Duplicate / Placement Notes?
```

---

### 17. Working Definition

The final atlas should follow this definition:

**A high-quality, technically categorized, functionally subdivided resource ocean for Computer Science, using short one-sentence annotations, sparse learning-value markers, clean official links, and explicit duplicate-control notes.**

{% endcomment %}
